            function createLink(src) {
                if ("connection" in navigator) {
                    if (navigator.connection.saveData != true) {
                        var preloadLink = document.createElement("link");
                        preloadLink.rel = "preload";
                        preloadLink.href = src;

                        preloadLink.type = "font/woff2";
                        preloadLink.crossOrigin = "";
                        document.head.insertBefore(preloadLink, document.head.firstChild);
                    }
                }
            }

<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41586"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Improved maize reference genome with single-molecule technologies"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature 2017 546:7659"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2017-06-12"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2017 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="An improved reference genome for maize, using single-molecule sequencing and high-resolution optical mapping, enables characterization of structural variation and repetitive regions, and identifies lineage expansions of transposable elements that are unique to maize. The maize genome was initially reported in 2009 but with some accuracy limitations. Doreen Ware and colleagues report a new reference genome for maize using single-molecule sequencing and high-resolution optical mapping. The technique shows improvements in the gene space including resolution of gaps and misassemblies and correction of order and orientation of genes. The authors characterize structural variation and repetitive regions, and identify transposable element lineage expansions unique to maize. Complete and accurate reference genomes and annotations provide fundamental tools for characterization of genetic and functional variation1. These resources facilitate the determination of biological processes and support translation of research findings into improved and sustainable agricultural technologies. Many reference genomes for crop plants have been generated over the past decade, but these genomes are often fragmented and missing complex repeat regions2. Here we report the assembly and annotation of a reference genome of maize, a genetic and agricultural model species, using single-molecule real-time sequencing and high-resolution optical mapping. Relative to the previous reference genome3, our assembly features a 52-fold increase in contig length and notable improvements in the assembly of intergenic spaces and centromeres. Characterization of the repetitive portion of the genome revealed more than 130,000 intact transposable elements, allowing us to identify transposable element lineage expansions that are unique to maize. Gene annotations were updated using 111,000 full-length transcripts obtained by single-molecule real-time sequencing4. In addition, comparative optical mapping of two other inbred maize lines revealed a prevalence of deletions in regions of low gene density and maize lineage-specific genes."/>

data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Jeffrey-Ross_Ibarra" data-author-popup="auth-Jeffrey-Ross_Ibarra">Jeffrey Ross-Ibarra</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff10">10</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Center for Population Biology, and Genome Center, University of California" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.27860.3b, 0000 0004 1936 9684, Department of Plant Sciences, Center for Population Biology, and Genome Center, University of California, Davis, 95616, California, USA" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-R__Kelly-Dawe" data-author-popup="auth-R__Kelly-Dawe">R. Kelly Dawe</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff6">6</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="University of Georgia" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.213876.9, 0000 0004 1936 738X, University of Georgia, Athens, 30602, Georgia, USA" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Alex-Hastie" data-author-popup="auth-Alex-Hastie">Alex Hastie</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff3">3</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="BioNano Genomics" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.470262.5, 0000 0004 0473 1353, BioNano Genomics, San Diego, 92121, California, USA" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-David_R_-Rank" data-author-popup="auth-David_R_-Rank">David R. Rank</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff2">2</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Pacific Biosciences" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.423340.2, Pacific Biosciences, Menlo Park, 94025, California, USA" /></span></sup> &amp; </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Doreen-Ware" data-author-popup="auth-Doreen-Ware" data-corresp-id="c1">Doreen Ware<svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-email"></use></svg></a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a>,<a href="#Aff11">11</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Cold Spring Harbor Laboratory, Cold Spring Harbor" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.225279.9, 0000 0004 0387 3667, Cold Spring Harbor Laboratory, Cold Spring Harbor, New York, 11724, USA" /></span><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="USDA-ARS, NEA Robert W. Holley Center for Agriculture and Health, Cornell University" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.5386.8, 000000041936877X, USDA-ARS, NEA Robert W. Holley Center for Agriculture and Health, Cornell University, Ithaca, 14853, New York, USA" /></span></sup> </li></ul>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs3" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs3-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs3">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs3-content"><p>Complete and accurate reference genomes and annotations provide fundamental tools for characterization of genetic and functional variation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Edwards, D., Batley, J. &amp; Snowdon, R. J. Accessing complex crop genomes with next-generation sequencing. Theor. Appl. Genet. 126, 1–11 (2013)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d45369e806">1</a></sup>. These resources facilitate the determination of biological processes and support translation of research findings into improved and sustainable agricultural technologies. Many reference genomes for crop plants have been generated over the past decade, but these genomes are often fragmented and missing complex repeat regions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Morrell, P. L., Buckler, E. S. &amp; Ross-Ibarra, J. Crop genomics: advances and applications. Nat. Rev. Genet. 13, 85–96 (2011)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d45369e810">2</a></sup>. Here we report the assembly and annotation of a reference genome of maize, a genetic and agricultural model species, using single-molecule real-time sequencing and high-resolution optical mapping. Relative to the previous reference genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Schnable, P. S. et al. The B73 maize genome: complexity, diversity, and dynamics. Science 326, 1112–1115 (2009)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d45369e814">3</a></sup>, our assembly features a 52-fold increase in contig length and notable improvements in the assembly of intergenic spaces and centromeres. Characterization of the repetitive portion of the genome revealed more than 130,000 intact transposable elements, allowing us to identify transposable element lineage expansions that are unique to maize. Gene annotations were updated using 111,000 full-length transcripts obtained by single-molecule real-time sequencing<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Wang, B. et al. Unveiling the complexity of the maize transcriptome by single-molecule long-read sequencing. Nat. Commun. 7, 11708 (2016)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d45369e818">4</a></sup>. In addition, comparative optical mapping of two other inbred maize lines revealed a prevalence of deletions in regions of low gene density and maize lineage-specific genes.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>
                
                    <div class="c-pdf-button__container">

            <span>Download PDF</span>

        </a>
    </div>
    

                        
                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Maize is the most productive and widely grown crop in the world, as well as a foundational model for genetics and genomics<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Hake, S. &amp; Ross-Ibarra, J. Genetic, evolutionary and plant breeding insights from the domestication of maize. eLife 4, (2015)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d45369e848">5</a></sup>. An accurate genome assembly for maize is crucial for all forms of basic and applied research, which will enable increases in yield to feed the growing world population. The current assembly of the maize genome, based on Sanger sequencing, was first published in 2009 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Schnable, P. S. et al. The B73 maize genome: complexity, diversity, and dynamics. Science 326, 1112–1115 (2009)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d45369e851">3</a>). Although this initial reference enabled rapid progress in maize genomics<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Edwards, D., Batley, J. &amp; Snowdon, R. J. Accessing complex crop genomes with next-generation sequencing. Theor. Appl. Genet. 126, 1–11 (2013)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d45369e855">1</a></sup>, the original assembly is composed of more than 100,000 small contigs, many of which are arbitrarily ordered and oriented, markedly complicating detailed analysis of individual loci<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Fouquet, R. et al. Maize rough endosperm3 encodes an RNA splicing factor required for endosperm cell differentiation and has a nonautonomous effect on embryo development. Plant Cell 23, 4280–4297 (2011)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d45369e859">6</a></sup> and impeding investigation of intergenic regions crucial to our understanding of phenotypic variation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Wallace, J. G. et al. Association mapping across numerous traits reveals patterns of functional variation in maize. PLoS Genet. 10, e1004845 (2014)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d45369e863">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Rodgers-Melnick, E., Vera, D. L., Bass, H. W. &amp; Buckler, E. S. Open chromatin reveals the functional maize genome. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, E3177–E3184 (2016)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d45369e866">8</a></sup> and genome evolution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Hufford, M. B. et al. Comparative population genomics of maize domestication and improvement. Nat. Genet. 44, 808–811 (2012)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d45369e871">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Chia, J. M. et al. Maize HapMap2 identifies extant variation from a genome in flux. Nat. Genet. 44, 803–807 (2012)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d45369e874">10</a></sup>.</p><p>Here we report a vastly improved <i>de novo</i> assembly and annotation of the maize reference genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22971#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>). On the basis of 65× single-molecule real-time sequencing (SMRT) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22971#Fig4">Extended Data Fig. 1</a>), we assembled the genome of the maize inbred line B73 into 2,958 contigs, in which half of the total assembly is made up of contigs larger than 1.2 Mb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nature22971#Tab1">Table 1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22971#Fig5">Extended Data Fig. 2a</a>). The assembly of the long reads was then integrated with a high-quality optical map (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22971#Fig4">Extended Data Fig. 1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nature22971#Tab1">Extended Data Table 1</a>) to create a hybrid assembly consisting of 625 scaffolds (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nature22971#Tab1">Table 1</a>). To build chromosome-level super-scaffolds, we combined the hybrid assembly with a minimum tiling path generated from the bacterial artificial chromosomes (BACs)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Wei, F. et al. The physical and genetic framework of the maize B73 genome. PLoS Genet. 5, e1000715 (2009)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d45369e906">11</a></sup> and a high-density genetic map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Ganal, M. W. et al. A large maize (Zea mays L.) SNP genotyping array: development and germplasm genotyping, and genetic mapping to compare with the B73 reference genome. PLoS One 6, e28334 (2011)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d45369e910">12</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22971#Fig5">Extended Data Fig. 2b</a>). After gap-filling and error correction using short sequence reads, the total size of maize B73 RefGen_v4 pseudomolecules was 2,106 Mb. The new reference assembly has 2,522 gaps, of which almost half (<i>n</i> = 1,115) have optical map coverage, giving an estimated mean gap length of 27 kb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22971#Fig5">Extended Data Fig. 2c</a>). The new maize B73 reference genome has 240-fold higher contiguity than the recently published short-read genome assembly of maize cultivar PH207 (contig N50: 1,180 kb versus 5 kb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Hirsch, C. N. et al. Draft assembly of elite inbred line PH207 provides insights into genomic and transcriptome diversity in maize. Plant Cell 28, 2700–2714 (2016)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d45369e924">13</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Genome assembly layout."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: Genome assembly layout.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature22971/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature22971/MediaObjects/41586_2017_BFnature22971_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature22971/MediaObjects/41586_2017_BFnature22971_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="272" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Workflow for genome construction. <b>b</b>, Ideograms of maize B73 version 4 reference pseudomolecules. The top track shows positions of 2,522 gaps in the pseudomolecules, including 1,115 gaps in which the lengths were estimated using optical genome maps (orange), whereas the remainder (purple) have undetermined lengths. More than half of the assembly is constituted of contigs longer than 1 Mb, which are shown as light grey bars in the bottom track.</p><p>
<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22971#MOESM2">PowerPoint slide</a>
</p><p>
<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22971#MOESM5">Source data</a>
</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature22971/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Assembly statistics of the maize B73 RefGen_v4 genome</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/nature22971/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Comparison of the new assembly to the previous BAC-based maize reference genome assembly revealed more than 99.9% sequence identity and a 52-fold increase in the mean contig length, with 84% of the BACs spanned by a single contig from the long reads assembly. Alignment of chromatin-immunoprecipitation followed by sequencing (ChIP–seq) data for the centromere-specific histone H3 (CENH3)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Gent, J. I., Wang, K., Jiang, J. &amp; Dawe, R. K. Stable patterns of CENH3 occupancy through maize lineages containing genetically similar centromeres. Genetics 200, 1105–1116 (2015)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d45369e1306">14</a></sup> revealed that centromeres are accurately placed and largely intact. Several previously identified<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Schneider, K. L., Xie, Z., Wolfgruber, T. K. &amp; Presting, G. G. Inbreeding drives maize centromere evolution. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, E987–E996 (2016)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d45369e1310">15</a></sup> megabase-sized mis-oriented pericentromeric regions were also corrected (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22971#Fig6">Extended Data Fig. 3a, b</a>). Moreover, the ends of the chromosomes are properly identified on 14 out of the 20 chromosome arms based on the presence of tandem telomeric repeats and knob 180 sequences (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22971#Fig6">Extended Data Fig. 3a, c</a>).</p><p>Our assembly made substantial improvements in the gene space including resolution of gaps and misassemblies and correction of order and orientation of genes. We also updated the annotation of our new assembly, resulting in consolidation of gene models (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22971#Fig7">Extended Data Fig. 4</a>). Newly published full-length cDNA data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Wang, B. et al. Unveiling the complexity of the maize transcriptome by single-molecule long-read sequencing. Nat. Commun. 7, 11708 (2016)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d45369e1326">4</a></sup> improved the annotation of alternative splicing by more than doubling the number of alternative transcripts from 1.6 to 3.3 per gene (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22971#Fig8">Extended Data Fig. 5a</a>), with about 70% of genes supported by the full-length transcripts. Our reference assembly also vastly improved the coverage of regulatory sequences, decreasing the number of genes exhibiting gaps in the 3-kb region(s) flanking coding sequence from 20% to &lt;1% (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22971#Fig8">Extended Data Fig. 5b</a>). The more complete sequence enabled notable improvements in the annotation of core promoter elements, especially the TATA-box, CCAAT-box, and Y patch motifs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22971#MOESM1">Supplementary Information</a>). Quantitative genetic analyses have shown that polymorphisms in regulatory regions explain a substantial majority of the genetic variation for many phenotypes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Wallace, J. G. et al. Association mapping across numerous traits reveals patterns of functional variation in maize. PLoS Genet. 10, e1004845 (2014)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d45369e1340">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Rodgers-Melnick, E., Vera, D. L., Bass, H. W. &amp; Buckler, E. S. Open chromatin reveals the functional maize genome. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, E3177–E3184 (2016)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d45369e1343">8</a></sup>, suggesting that the new reference will markedly improve our ability to identify and predict functional genetic variation.</p><p>After its divergence from <i>Sorghum</i>, the maize lineage underwent genome doubling followed by diploidization and gene loss. Previous work showed that gene loss is biased towards one of the parental genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Schnable, P. S. et al. The B73 maize genome: complexity, diversity, and dynamics. Science 326, 1112–1115 (2009)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d45369e1354">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Schnable, J. C., Springer, N. M. &amp; Freeling, M. Differentiation of the maize subgenomes by genome dominance and both ancient and ongoing gene loss. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 4069–4074 (2011)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d45369e1357">16</a></sup>, but our new assembly and annotation instead suggest that 56% of syntenic sorghum orthologues map uniquely to the dominant maize subgenome (designated A, total size 1.16 Gb), whereas only 24% map uniquely to subgenome B (total size 0.63 Gb). Gene loss in maize has primarily been considered in the context of polyploidy and functional redundancy<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Schnable, J. C., Springer, N. M. &amp; Freeling, M. Differentiation of the maize subgenomes by genome dominance and both ancient and ongoing gene loss. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 4069–4074 (2011)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d45369e1361">16</a></sup>, but we found that despite its polyploidy, maize has lost a larger proportion (14%) of the 22,048 ancestral gene orthologues than any of the other four grass species evaluated to date (<i>Sorghum</i>, rice, <i>Brachypodium distachyon</i> and <i>Setaria italica</i>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22971#Fig9">Extended Data Fig. 6</a>). Nearly one-third of these losses are specific to maize, and analysis of a restricted high-confidence set revealed enrichment for genes involved in biotic and abiotic stresses (Extended Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nature22971#Tab2">Table 2</a>), for example, NB-ARC domain disease-resistance genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="McHale, L., Tan, X., Koehl, P. &amp; Michelmore, R. W. Plant NBS-LRR proteins: adaptable guards. Genome Biol. 7, 212 (2006)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d45369e1381">17</a></sup> and the serpin protease inhibitor involved in pathogen defence and programmed cell death<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Fluhr, R., Lampl, N. &amp; Roberts, T. H. Serpin protease inhibitors in plant biology. Physiol. Plant. 145, 95–102 (2012)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d45369e1385">18</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 Structural variations from optical maps of two maize lines</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/nature22971/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Transposable elements were first reported in maize<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="McClintock, B. The origin and behavior of mutable loci in maize. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 36, 344–355 (1950)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d45369e1615">19</a></sup> and have since been shown to have important roles in shaping genome evolution and gene regulatory networks of many species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Slotkin, R. K. &amp; Martienssen, R. Transposable elements and the epigenetic regulation of the genome. Nat. Rev. Genet. 8, 272–285 (2007)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d45369e1619">20</a></sup>. Most of the maize genome is derived from transposable elements<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Schnable, P. S. et al. The B73 maize genome: complexity, diversity, and dynamics. Science 326, 1112–1115 (2009)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d45369e1623">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="SanMiguel, P. et al. Nested retrotransposons in the intergenic regions of the maize genome. Science 274, 765–768 (1996)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d45369e1626">21</a></sup>, and careful study of a few regions has revealed a characteristic structure of sequentially nested retrotransposons<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="SanMiguel, P. et al. Nested retrotransposons in the intergenic regions of the maize genome. Science 274, 765–768 (1996)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d45369e1630">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Brunner, S., Fengler, K., Morgante, M., Tingey, S. &amp; Rafalski, A. Evolution of DNA sequence nonhomologies among maize inbreds. Plant Cell 17, 343–360 (2005)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d45369e1633">22</a></sup> and the effect of deletions and recombination on retrotransposon evolution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Sharma, A., Schneider, K. L. &amp; Presting, G. G. Sustained retrotransposition is mediated by nucleotide deletions and interelement recombinations. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 105, 15470–15474 (2008)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d45369e1637">23</a></sup>. In the annotation of the original maize assembly, however, fewer than 1% of long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposon copies were intact<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Baucom, R. S. et al. Exceptional diversity, non-random distribution, and rapid evolution of retroelements in the B73 maize genome. PLoS Genet. 5, e1000732 (2009)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d45369e1642">24</a></sup>. By applying a new homology-independent annotation pipeline to our assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nature22971#Tab3">Extended Data Table 3</a>), we identified 1,268 Mb (130,604 copies) of structurally intact retrotransposons, of which 661 Mb (70,035 copies) are nested retrotransposon copies disrupted by the insertion of other transposable elements, 8.7 Mb (14,041 copies) are DNA terminal inverted repeat transposons, and 76 Mb (21,095 copies) are helitrons. To understand the evolutionary history of maize LTR retrotransposons, we also applied our annotation pipeline to the sorghum reference genome, and used reverse transcriptase protein domain sequences that were accessible owing to the improved assembly of the internal protein coding domains of maize LTR retrotransposons to reconstruct the phylogeny of maize and sorghum LTR retrotransposon families. Despite a higher overall rate of diversification of LTR transposable elements in the maize lineage consistent with its larger genome size, differences in LTR retrotransposon content between genomes were primarily the result of marked expansion of distinct families in both lineages (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22971#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Phylogeny of maize and sorghum LTR retrotransposon families."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Phylogeny of maize and sorghum LTR retrotransposon families.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature22971/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature22971/MediaObjects/41586_2017_BFnature22971_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature22971/MediaObjects/41586_2017_BFnature22971_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="320" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, <b>b</b>, Both Ty3/Gypsy (<b>a</b>) and Ty1/Copia (<b>b</b>) superfamilies are present at higher copy number in maize (red) than in sorghum (blue). Bars (log<sub>10</sub>-scaled) depict family copy numbers.</p><p>
<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22971#MOESM3">PowerPoint slide</a>
</p><p>
<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22971#MOESM6">Source data</a>
</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature22971/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Maize exhibits tremendous genetic diversity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Buckler, E. S., Gaut, B. S. &amp; McMullen, M. D. Molecular and functional diversity of maize. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 9, 172–176 (2006)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d45369e1700">25</a></sup>, and both nucleotide polymorphisms and structural variations have important roles in its phenotypic variation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Chia, J. M. et al. Maize HapMap2 identifies extant variation from a genome in flux. Nat. Genet. 44, 803–807 (2012)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d45369e1704">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Dooner, H. K. &amp; He, L. Maize genome structure variation: interplay between retrotransposon polymorphisms and genic recombination. Plant Cell 20, 249–258 (2008)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d45369e1707">26</a></sup>. However, genome-wide patterns of structural variation in plant genomes are difficult to assess<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Saxena, R. K., Edwards, D. &amp; Varshney, R. K. Structural variations in plant genomes. Brief. Funct. Genomics 13, 296–307 (2014)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d45369e1711">27</a></sup>, and previous efforts have relied on short-read mapping, which misses the vast majority of intergenic spaces where most rearrangements are likely to occur<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Chia, J. M. et al. Maize HapMap2 identifies extant variation from a genome in flux. Nat. Genet. 44, 803–807 (2012)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d45369e1715">10</a></sup>. To investigate structural variation at a genome-wide scale, we generated optical maps (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nature22971#Tab1">Extended Data Table 1</a>) for two additional maize inbred lines: the tropical line Ki11, one of the founders of the maize nested association mapping (NAM) population<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="McMullen, M. D. et al. Genetic properties of the maize nested association mapping population. Science 325, 737–740 (2009)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d45369e1723">28</a></sup>, and W22, which has served as a foundation for studies of maize genetics<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Strable, J . &amp; Scanlon, M. J. Maize (Zea mays): a model organism for basic and applied research in plant biology. Cold Spring Harb. Protoc. 2009, pdb.emo132 (2009)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d45369e1727">29</a></sup>. Owing to the high degree of genomic diversity among these lines, only 32% of the assembled 2,216 Mb map of Ki11 and 39% of the 2,280 Mb W22 map could be mapped to our new B73 reference via common restriction patterns (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nature22971#Tab2">Table 2</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22971#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22971#Fig10">Extended Data Fig. 7</a>). The high density of alignments across and near many of the exceedingly retrotransposon-rich centromeres reflects the comparatively low genetic diversity of most centromeres in domesticated maize<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Schneider, K. L., Xie, Z., Wolfgruber, T. K. &amp; Presting, G. G. Inbreeding drives maize centromere evolution. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, E987–E996 (2016)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d45369e1740">15</a></sup> and illustrates the ability of the combined optical mapping/single-molecule sequencing methodology to traverse large repeat-rich regions. Within the aligned regions, approximately 32% of the Ki11 and 26% of the W22 optical maps exhibited clear evidence of structural variation, including 3,408 insertions and 3,298 deletions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nature22971#Tab2">Table 2</a>). The average indel size was approximately 20 kb, with a range from 1 kb to over 1 Mb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22971#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a>). More than 90% of the indels were unique to one inbred or the other, indicating a high level of structural diversity in maize. As short-read sequence data are available from both Ki11 and W22 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Chia, J. M. et al. Maize HapMap2 identifies extant variation from a genome in flux. Nat. Genet. 44, 803–807 (2012)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d45369e1750">10</a>), we analysed 1,451 of the largest (&gt;10 kb) deletions and found that 1,083 were supported by a clear reduction in read depth (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22971#Fig3">Fig. 3c</a>). The confirmed deletions occurred in regions of low gene density (4.4 genes per megabase compared to a genome-wide average of 18.7 genes per megabase). One-third (83 out of 257) of the genes missing in Ki11 or W22 lack putative orthologues in all four grasses (rice, sorghum, <i>Brachypodium</i> and <i>Setaria</i>), consistent with previous data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Swanson-Wagner, R. A. et al. Pervasive gene content variation and copy number variation in maize and its undomesticated progenitor. Genome Res. 20, 1689–1699 (2010)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d45369e1764">30</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Structural variation from Ki11 and W22."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Structural variation from Ki11 and W22.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature22971/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature22971/MediaObjects/41586_2017_BFnature22971_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature22971/MediaObjects/41586_2017_BFnature22971_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="404" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Alignment and structural variation called from Ki11 and W22 optical maps on chromosome 10. <b>b</b>, Size distribution of the insertion and deletions in Ki11 and W22. <b>c</b>, Example of using short-read alignment to verify a missing region mapped in Ki11.</p><p>
<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22971#MOESM4">PowerPoint slide</a>
</p><p>
<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22971#MOESM7">Source data</a>
</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature22971/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Although maize is often considered to be a large-genome crop, most major food crops have even larger genomes with more complex repeat landscapes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Morrell, P. L., Buckler, E. S. &amp; Ross-Ibarra, J. Crop genomics: advances and applications. Nat. Rev. Genet. 13, 85–96 (2011)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d45369e1811">2</a></sup>. Our improved assembly of the B73 genome, generated using single-molecule technologies, demonstrates that additional assemblies of other maize inbred lines and similar high-quality assemblies of other repeat-rich and large-genome plants are feasible. Further high-quality assemblies will in turn extend our understanding of the genetic diversity that forms the basis of the phenotypic diversity in maize and other economically important plants.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><p>No statistical methods were used to predetermine sample size. The experiments were not randomized, and investigators were not blinded to allocation during experiments and outcome assessment.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Whole-genome sequencing using SMRT technology</h3><p>DNA samples for SMRT sequencing were prepared using maize inbred line B73 from NCRPIS (PI550473), grown at University of Missouri. Seeds of this line were deposited at NCRPIS (tracking number PI677128). Etiolated seedlings were grown for 4–6 days in Pro-Mix at 37 °C in darkness to minimize chloroplast DNA. Batches of ~10 g were snap-frozen in liquid nitrogen. DNA was extracted following the PacBio protocol ‘Preparing Arabidopsis Genomic DNA for Size-Selected ~20 kb SMRTbell Libraries’ (<a href="http://www.pacb.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/Shared-Protocol-Preparing-Arabidopsis-DNA-for-20-kb-SMRTbell-Libraries.pdf">http://www.pacb.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/Shared-Protocol-Preparing-Arabidopsis-DNA-for-20-kb-SMRTbell-Libraries.pdf</a>).</p><p>Genomic DNA was sheared to a size range of 15–40 kb using either G-tubes (Covaris) or a Megarupter device (Diagenode), and enzymatically repaired and converted into SMRTbell template libraries as recommended by Pacific Biosciences. In brief, hairpin adapters were ligated, after which the remaining damaged DNA fragments and those without adapters at both ends were eliminated by digestion with exonucleases. The resulting SMRTbell templates were size-selected by Blue Pippin electrophoresis (Sage Sciences) and templates ranging from 15 to 50 kb, were sequenced on a PacBio RS II instrument using P6-C4 sequencing chemistry. To acquire long reads, all data were collected as either 5- or 6-h sequencing videos.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Construction of optical genome maps using the Irys system</h3><p>High-molecular mass genomic DNA was isolated from 3 g of young ear tissue after fixing with 2% formaldehyde. Nuclei were purified and lysed in embedded agarose as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="VanBuren, R. et al. Single-molecule sequencing of the desiccation-tolerant grass Oropetium thomaeum. Nature 527, 508–511 (2015)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d45369e1846">31</a></sup>. DNA was labelled at Nt.BspQI sites using the IrysPrep kit. Molecules collected from BioNano chips were <i>de novo</i> assembled as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Pendleton, M. et al. Assembly and diploid architecture of an individual human genome via single-molecule technologies. Nat. Methods 12, 780–786 (2015)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d45369e1853">32</a></sup> using ‘optArgument_human’.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5"><i>De novo</i> assembly of the genome sequencing data</h3><p><i>De novo</i> assembly of the long reads from SMRT Sequencing was performed using two assemblers: the Celera Assembler PBcR –MHAP pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Berlin, K. et al. Assembling large genomes with single-molecule sequencing and locality-sensitive hashing. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 623–630 (2015)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d45369e1870">33</a></sup> and Falcon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Chin, C. S. et al. Phased diploid genome assembly with single-molecule real-time sequencing. Nat. Methods 13, 1050–1054 (2016)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d45369e1874">34</a></sup> with different parameter settings. Quiver from SMRT Analysis v2.3.0 was used to polish base calling of contigs. The three independent assemblies were evaluated by aligning with the optical genome maps.</p><p>Contamination of contigs by bacterial and plasmid genomes was eliminated using the NCBI GenBank submission system<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Clark, K., Karsch-Mizrachi, I., Lipman, D. J., Ostell, J. &amp; Sayers, E. W. GenBank. Nucleic Acids Res. 44, D67–D72 (2016)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d45369e1881">35</a></sup>. Curation of the assembly, including resolution of conflicts between the contigs and the optical map and removal of redundancy at the edges of contigs, is described in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22971#MOESM1">Supplementary Information</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Hybrid scaffold construction</h3><p>To create hybrid scaffolds, curated sequence contigs and optical maps were aligned and merged with RefAligner<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Pendleton, M. et al. Assembly and diploid architecture of an individual human genome via single-molecule technologies. Nat. Methods 12, 780–786 (2015)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d45369e1897">32</a></sup> (<i>P</i> &lt; 1 × 10<sup>−11</sup>). These initial hybrid scaffolds were aligned again to the sequence contigs using a less stringent <i>P</i> value (1 × 10<sup>−8</sup>), and those contigs not previously merged were added if they aligned over 50% of their length and without overlapping previously merged contigs, thereby generating final hybrid scaffolds.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Pseudomolecule construction</h3><p>Sequences from BACs on the physical map that were used to build the maize v3 pseudomolecules were aligned to contigs using MUMMER package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Kurtz, S. et al. Versatile and open software for comparing large genomes. Genome Biol. 5, R12 (2004)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d45369e1919">36</a></sup> with the following parameter settings: ‘-l(minimum length of a single match) 100 -c(the minimum length of a cluster of matches) 1000’. To only use unique hits as markers, alignment hits were filtered with the following parameters: ‘-i(the minimum alignment identity) 98 -l(the minimum alignment length) 10000’. Scaffolds were then ordered and oriented into pseudochromosomes using the order of BACs as a guide. For quality control, we mapped the SNP markers from a genetic map built from an intermated maize recombinant inbred line population (Mo17 × B73)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Chia, J. M. et al. Maize HapMap2 identifies extant variation from a genome in flux. Nat. Genet. 44, 803–807 (2012)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d45369e1923">10</a></sup>. Contigs with markers not located in pseudochromosomes from the physical map were placed into the AGP (A Golden Path) using the genetic map.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Further polishing of pseudomolecules</h3><p>Raw pseudomolecules were subjected to gap filling using Pbjelly (-maxTrim = 0, -minReads = 2) and polished again using Quiver (SMRT Analysis v2.3.0). To increase the accuracy of the base calls, we performed two lanes of sequencing on the same genomic DNA sample (library size = 450 bp) using Illumina 2500 Rapid run, which generated about 100-fold 2 × 250 paired-end (PE) data. Reads were aligned to the assembly using BWA-mem<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754–1760 (2009)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d45369e1935">37</a></sup>. Sequence error correction was performed with the Pilon pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Walker, B. J. et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS One 9, e112963 (2014)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d45369e1939">38</a></sup>, after aligning reads with BWA-mem<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754–1760 (2009)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d45369e1943">37</a></sup> and parsing with SAMtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Li, H. et al. The Sequence Alignment/Map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078–2079 (2009)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d45369e1947">39</a></sup>, using sequence and alignment quality scores above 20.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Annotation</h3><p>For comprehensive annotation of transposable elements, we designed a structural identification pipeline incorporating several tools, including LTRharvest<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Ellinghaus, D., Kurtz, S. &amp; Willhoeft, U. LTRharvest, an efficient and flexible software for de novo detection of LTR retrotransposons. BMC Bioinformatics 9, 18 (2008)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d45369e1959">40</a></sup>, LTRdigest<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Steinbiss, S., Willhoeft, U., Gremme, G. &amp; Kurtz, S. Fine-grained annotation and classification of de novo predicted LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 37, 7002–7013 (2009)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d45369e1963">41</a></sup>, SINE-Finder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Wenke, T. et al. Targeted identification of short interspersed nuclear element families shows their widespread existence and extreme heterogeneity in plant genomes. Plant Cell 23, 3117–3128 (2011)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d45369e1967">42</a></sup>, MGEScan-non-LTR<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Rho, M. &amp; Tang, H. MGEScan-non-LTR: computational identification and classification of autonomous non-LTR retrotransposons in eukaryotic genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 37, e143 (2009)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d45369e1971">43</a></sup>, MITE-hunter<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Han, Y. &amp; Wessler, S. R. MITE-Hunter: a program for discovering miniature inverted-repeat transposable elements from genomic sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 38, e199 (2010)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d45369e1975">44</a></sup>, HelitronScanner<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Xiong, W., He, L., Lai, J., Dooner, H. K. &amp; Du, C. HelitronScanner uncovers a large overlooked cache of Helitron transposons in many plant genomes. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 111, 10263–10268 (2014)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d45369e1980">45</a></sup>, and others (details in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22971#MOESM1">Supplementary Information</a>). The scripts, parameters, and intermediate files of each transposable element superfamily are available at <a href="https://github.com/mcstitzer/maize_v4_TE_annotation">https://github.com/mcstitzer/maize_v4_TE_annotation</a>.</p><p>The MAKER-P pipeline was used to annotate protein-coding genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Campbell, M. S. et al. MAKER-P: a tool kit for the rapid creation, management, and quality control of plant genome annotations. Plant Physiol. 164, 513–524 (2014)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d45369e1997">46</a></sup>, integrating <i>ab initio</i> prediction with publicly available evidence from full-length cDNA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Soderlund, C. et al. Sequencing, mapping, and analysis of 27,455 maize full-length cDNAs. PLoS Genet. 5, e1000740 (2009)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d45369e2004">47</a></sup>, <i>de novo</i> assembled transcripts from short-read mRNA sequencing (mRNA-seq)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Law, M. et al. Automated update, revision, and quality control of the maize genome annotations using MAKER-P improves the B73 RefGen_v3 gene models and identifies new genes. Plant Physiol. 167, 25–39 (2015)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d45369e2011">48</a></sup>, isoform-sequencing (Iso-Seq) full-length transcripts<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Gent, J. I., Wang, K., Jiang, J. &amp; Dawe, R. K. Stable patterns of CENH3 occupancy through maize lineages containing genetically similar centromeres. Genetics 200, 1105–1116 (2015)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d45369e2016">14</a></sup>, and proteins from other species. The gene models were filtered to remove transposons and low-confidence predictions. Additional alternative transcript isoforms were obtained from the Iso-Seq data. Further details on annotations, core promoter analysis, and comparative phylogenomics are described in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22971#MOESM1">Supplementary Information</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Structural variation</h3><p>Leaves were used to prepare high molecular mass DNA and optical genome maps were constructed as described above for B73. Structural variant calls were generated based on alignment to the reference map B73 v4 chromosomal assembly using the multiple local alignment algorithm (RefSplit)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Pendleton, M. et al. Assembly and diploid architecture of an individual human genome via single-molecule technologies. Nat. Methods 12, 780–786 (2015)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d45369e2031">32</a></sup>. A structural variant was identified as an alignment outlier<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Pendleton, M. et al. Assembly and diploid architecture of an individual human genome via single-molecule technologies. Nat. Methods 12, 780–786 (2015)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d45369e2035">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Mostovoy, Y. et al. A hybrid approach for de novo human genome sequence assembly and phasing. Nat. Methods 13, 587–590 (2016)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d45369e2038">49</a></sup>, defined as two well-aligned regions separated by a poorly aligned region with a large size difference between the reference genome and the map or by one or more unaligned sites, or alternatively as a gap between two local alignments. A confidence score was generated by comparing the non-normalized <i>P</i> values of the two well-aligned regions and the non-normalized log-likelihood ratio<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Cao, H. et al. Rapid detection of structural variation in a human genome using nanochannel-based genome mapping technology. Gigascience 3, 34 (2014)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d45369e2045">50</a></sup> of the unaligned or poorly aligned region. With a confidence score threshold of 3, RefSplit is sensitive to insertions and deletions as small as 100 bp (events smaller than 1 kb are generally compound or substitution and include label changes, not just spacing differences) and other changes such as inversions and complex events which could be balanced. Insertion and deletion calls were based on an alignment outlier <i>P</i>-value threshold of 1 × 10<sup>−4</sup>. Insertions or deletions that crossed gaps in the B73 pseudomolecules, or that were heterozygous in the optical genome maps, were excluded. Considering the resolution of the BioNano optical map, only insertion and deletions larger than 100 bp were used for subsequent analyses. To obtain high-confidence deletion sequences, sequencing reads from the maize HapMap2 project<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Rodgers-Melnick, E., Vera, D. L., Bass, H. W. &amp; Buckler, E. S. Open chromatin reveals the functional maize genome. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, E3177–E3184 (2016)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d45369e2055">8</a></sup> for Ki11 and W22 were aligned to our new B73 v4 reference genome using Bowtie2 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Langmead, B. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Fast gapped-read alignment with Bowtie 2. Nat. Methods 9, 357–359 (2012)" href="/articles/nature22971#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d45369e2058">51</a>). Read depth (minimum mapping quality &gt;20) was calculated in 10-kb windows with step size of 1 kb. Windows with read depth below 10 in Ki11 and 20 in W22 (sequencing depths for Ki11 and W22 were 2.32× and 4.04×, respectively) in the deleted region were retained for further analysis.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Data availability</h3><p>Raw reads, genome assembly sequences, and gene annotations have been deposited at the NCBI under BioProject number <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA10769">PRJNA10769</a> and BioSample number <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/biosample/?term=SAMN04296295">SAMN04296295</a>. PacBio whole-genome sequencing data and Illumina data were deposited in the NCBI SRA database under accessions <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRX1472849">SRX1472849</a> and <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRX1452310">SRX1452310</a>, respectively. The GenBank accession number of the genome assembly and annotation is <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=LPUQ00000000">LPUQ00000000</a>. A genome browser including genome feature tracks and ftp is available from Gramene: <a href="http://ensembl.gramene.org/Zea_mays/Info/Index">http://ensembl.gramene.org/Zea_mays/Info/Index</a>. All other data are available from the corresponding author upon reasonable request.</p></div></div></section>

                <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading">Competing interests</h3>
                <p>P.P., C.-S.C. and D.R.R. are full-time employees of Pacific Biosciences. J.S., T.L. and A.H. are employees of BioNano Genomics, Inc., and own company stock options. W.R.M. has participated in Illumina sponsored meetings over the past four years and received travel reimbursement and an honorarium for presenting at these events. Illumina had no role in decisions relating to the study/work to be published, data collection and analysis of data, or the decision to publish. W.R.M. has participated in Pacific Biosciences sponsored meetings over the past three years and received travel reimbursement for presenting at these events. W.R.M. is a founder and shared holder of Orion Genomics, which focuses on plant genomics and cancer genetics. W.R.M. is an SAB member for RainDance Technologies, Inc. All other authors declare no competing financial interests.</p>

A total of 150 Gb (~60-fold coverage) of single-molecule raw data from BioNano chips was collected for map construction. The N50 of the single molecules was ~261 kb, and the label density was 11.6 per 100 kb. After assembly, the total size of the map reached 2.12 Gb with an N50 of 2.47 Mb. <b>b</b>, Length distribution of SMRT sequencing reads. Sequencing of 212 P6-C4 SMRT cells on the PacBio platform generated ~65-fold depth-of-coverage of the nuclear genome. Read lengths averaged 11.7 kb, with reads above 10 kb providing 53-fold depth-of-coverage. <b>c</b>, The accuracy of SMRT sequencing from a representative run. The sequencing error rate was estimated at 10% from the alignment with the maize B73 RefGen_v3 by BLASR. <b>d</b>, Plot of the fraction of alignable data per run (alignable bases/total bases per chip) versus total raw bases (per chip) for each B73 sequencing run. As the plot shows, the trend in the data suggests that as the overall per run raw base yield increases, the fraction of alignable bases decreases. This is owing to the fact that in all runs, a subset of the zero-mode waveguide (ZMWs) will initially have more than one active sequencing enzyme in the observation field at the start of the sequencing run. A ZMW with more than one active polymerase will create unalignable bases while the two polymerases are simultaneously synthesizing DNA and yield a ‘merged sequencing signal from two independent polymerases’. As the loading of a chips increases (yield of bases), the probability of having two or more polymerases in a single ZMW increases.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig5"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary pseudomolecules.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p><b>a</b>, Summary of the three assembly sets. <b>b</b>, How the scaffolds were of the maize B73 RefGen_v4 genes with other grasses.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p><b>a</b>, Species-membership in orthologue sets, giving counts and percentage of orthologue sets of which each species is a member. Numbers in parentheses give the percentage of orthologue sets with membership of all species and versions within the clade. na, not applicable. <b>b</b>, Venn diagram showing overlap of 6,539 orthologue sets rooted in the Poaceae (true grasses) that are deficient in gene membership among five species.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig10"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/nature22971/figures/10" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature22971/MediaObjects/41586_2017_BFnature22971_Fig10_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Figure 7 Structural variation characterized from the Ki11 and W22 optical maps.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>

            <aside><div class="c-article-editorial-summary__container" data-component-show-more="true" id="editorial-summary"><section><h1 class="c-article-editorial-summary__title u-h3">Editorial Summary</h1><h2 class="c-article-editorial-summary__article-title">A better map of the maize genome</h2><div class="c-article-editorial-summary__content c-article-editorial-summary__content--less"><p>The maize genome was initially reported in 2009 but with some accuracy limitations. Doreen Ware and colleagues report a new reference genome for maize using single-molecule sequencing and high-resolution optical mapping. The technique shows improvements in the gene space including resolution of gaps and misassemblies and correction of order and orientation of genes. The authors characterize structural variation and repetitive regions, and identify transposable element lineage expansions unique to maize.</p></div></section></div></aside>
